Luo Longji
A famed scholar of the new era of intellectuals and a child prodigy, Luo Longji started his academic career in the Tsinghua Prep School for Studying in the United States in 1913. In 1919, he became a leading figure of the November Riot's Student Movement, and finally travelled to study in the United States in 1921. Entering the University of Wisconsin and later Columbia University to study political science, he graduated and entered the London School of Economics and Political Science due to his personal fascination over Laski, obtaining a doctorate in the field and becoming the latter's favourite student.\n\nReturning to China in 1928, he started to teach at Shanghai Guanghua University, and founded the Crescent Moon bookstore and magazine, serving as the editor-in-chief. Writing political publications in what was nominally intended to be a literature and prose analysis journal, he would draw much ire and strain relations with his more writing inclined fellows, such as Xu Zhimo, but nonetheless carried on his crusade against authoritarianism within the nation.\n\nIn 1933 Luo was almost assassinated due to his inherent controversy, and was dismissed from the Yi Shi Bao publication, fleeing into the interior. Promoting a humanistic, liberal, yet modern conservative vision for China inspired by Anglo-American writers and systems, Luo Longji serves as the informal face of the Crescent Moon Society, a gathering of his friends that transformed into a political outlet.
§YBorn:§! §LJuly 30th, 1896 in Anfu County, Jiangxi Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$YUN_luo_longji_bio$§!
§YBorn:§! §LJuly 30th, 1896 in Anfu County, Jiangxi Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §lCrescent Moon Society§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$YUN_luo_longji_bio$§!\n\n§YNotes: He is another close friend of Hu Shih.§!
Cun Shusheng
Privately educated at a young age, Cun Shusheng was a diligent and active student, imaginative and praised by his Principal for his ambition. In 1918 he travelled to Japan to study at the East Asia Preparatory School, Tokyo Higher Normal School, and the Economics Department of the Faculty of Arts and Letters at Kyushu Imperial University.\n\nGraduating with a bachelor's in economics, he joined the Creation Society during his time in Japan, and in 1928 joined the Social Science Research Society, organised by Chinese students studying in Japan and Taiwan. Serving as their chief writer, he researched and promoted socialist theory, even stewarding one Ai Siqi. With encroaching foreign influence upon China, he finally left Japan in 1931 to teach at the Law and Business School in Beijing University.\n\nFollowing the League Uprising and intellectual crackdown, Cun retreated into the interior, later participating in Xing Xiping's World Forum, and joining the National Salvation Federation through Ma Xulun. Invited to Yunnan by Luo Longji, he became a rallying point for the left-wing of the Crescent Moon Society. Coveting an alliance with socialists, he has found himself at amicable odds with the Liberal Conservatives of the Society, but continues to insist on unity.

Su Honggang
§YBorn:§! §L1878, in Jinning, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §lCrescent Moon Society§!\n§YBackground:§! §LStudying diligently as a child, Su Honggang was taught to always be kind in spite of any difficulties, and such a teaching would stay with him, profoundly impacting his life and mindset. Passing the imperial exam at the age of 16, he would teach at a local primary school, and be admitted to the Yunnan Provincial Higher School in 1903. In 1908 he participated in the Yunwu Alumni Association in Kunming, engaging in anti-Qing activities. During Xinhai, Su would become dedicated to the cause of people's enlightenment through education, and with the final approval of the Minister of Education in 1919k established a private school.\n\nRunning the school in a caring, thoughtful, and egalitarian manner, it would expand to encompass two middle school classes, and two primary school classes, with an additional junior high school department. Serving as principal, he would teach a whole generation of Yunnanese, without taking an additional salary.\n\nWith the retreat of various scholars into the interior, Su begun to associate with their reformist and progressive circles, becoming an informal member of the Crescent Moon Society in the Southwest. Hesitant to play politics however, he is far more keen to focus on education, social issues, and human rights.§!\n\n§YNotes: He is a close friend of Wen Yiduo and Li Gongpu.§!

During November 4th, Liang was an active participant in the school movement. In 1923 he went to the United States to study at Colorado College, and in 1924 would graduate to study at Harvard. On the way he co-founded the nationalist writer's group the Dajiang Society with Wen Yiduo, Luo Longji, He Haoruo, Wu Jingchao and others.\n\nStudying for a master's degree at Harvard University, he would be influenced by Babbitt's New Humanism, and received a Ph.D. in philosophy. Returning to China in 1926, he would teach at National Southeast University, Qingdao University, and director of the Foreign Language Department alongside the library of Beijing Normal Unviersity. In 1927 he would found the Crescent Moon Bookstore with Xu Zhimo and Wen Yiduo, and would teach at Jinan University.\n\nBetween 1927 and 1936 Liang would engage in a series of intellectual debates with various left-wing writers, namely Lu Xun, due to his critical stance on class theory and promotion of modern conservatism. Following the Shanghai Uprising, Liang would enter the interior of his own volition.§!\n\n§YNotes: He is a close friend of Hu Shih, and the two exchange dialogue about their plans to translate Shakespeare.\nHe has been insulted by Lu Xun as merely \

He Haoruo

Later He would enter the Lowe Military Academy to study cavalry, and in 1926 returned to China to serve as a instructor at the Whampoa Military Academy. Having been associated with the Dajiang Society during his time the United States, He would find himself reunited with his old colleagues in their new Crescent Moon Society, notably as one of the few members with any military experience.§!
Long Yun
A member of the Yi ethnic group and born into a landlord family with a slave-holder legacy, Long Yun could not learn to read at a young age, and so was sent to Zhaotong with his cousin, Lu Han, to study. Meeting Zuo Ruoheng, the three would become known as the \

§YBorn:§! §LNovember 19th, 1884 in Zhaotong, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §gLong Yun's Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$YUN_long_yun_bio$§!\n\n§YNotes: A moderniser first and foremost, Long has been known to flirt with Third Way democrats to bat against Tang Jiyao.§!
Lu Han
A cousin and childhood friend of Long Yun, Lu Han would join Wei Huanzhang's army in Sichuan with Long, participating in the Xinhai Revolution. Admitted to the fourth infantry class of the Kunming military academy in 1912 with Long, he would graduate in 1914 and be assigned to the Yunnan Army. Fighting in the National Protection War, he would see service in Sichuan under Cai E, and afterwards would serve as a platoon leader in the Yunnan Army stationed in Sichuan. Following the expulsion of the Yunnan army in 1920, he would serve as a major adjutant in Tang Jiyao's Governor's office, and would resolutely support him in light of Gu Pinzhen's coup.\n\nWith Tang's return Lu would become a commander of Tang's personal guard, and would fight in Guangxi under the command of Long Yun. Almost completely encircled, Lu Han would work with Hu Ruoyu, Zhang Ruji, Li Xuanting and Long Yun in an attempt to coup Tang in 1927, but due to its failure would be forced into Zhaotong. Sitting idly, he was considered to be Long Yun's most premier subordinate.

The daring general then betrayed Hu Ruoyu in turn, allowing him to seize control using his relative's former resources.§!

Zhang Chong
§YBorn:§! §LJanuary 25th, 1901, in Luxi County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §gLong Yun's Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LA member of the Yi ethnic group, the young Zhang Chong attended a private school, and later a primary school in his local county. Immersed in revolutionary sentiment from a young age, Zhang had developed a strong sympathy for the peasantry and working class, fighting in a local militia against bandits. Unfortunately due to his anti-landlord sentiments, he would be almost arrested in 1918, and forced into a life of banditry. More akin to a vigilante, fighting against local tyrants and evil gentry, he would become something of a local folk hero, and managed to trump provincial forces numerous times.\n\nInstead recruited by Tang Jiyao, he would serve as a regiment commander in the Yunnan army. Supported the coup attempt against Tang Jiyao by Long Yun, Hu Ruoyu, Zhang Ruji and Li Xuanting, Zhang was floated for the position of commander of the 5th division, but due to the failure of the coup was instead forced into Zhaotong with the rest of Long Yun's acolytes. Aiming to maintain his populist streak and aid the people, Zhang engaged in local reform efforts around Zhaotong, intending to turn it into somewhat of a model county.§!
Li Peiyan

In 1917 he was elected as the second provincial senator and concurrently served as the general manager of Luliang County, albeit he was later dismissed due to allegations of corruption and was imprisoned.\n\nIn 1922, after his sister Li Peilian married Long Yun, he was released from prison and resumed his duties. Appointed director of the Kaiguang Border Salt Bureau by Yuan Jiagu, Li would resolutely stand by Long Yun during the 1927 coup rumbling, and whilst floated for the position of President of the Yunnan Fudian New Bank, he would instead be shuffled to that of Yunnan Salt Transport Commissioner. Building a number of water conservation, education, health and industrial facilities in Binchuan, he has slowly amassed wealth and power within Yunnan.§!\n\n§YNotes: He is indirectly related to Long Yun as his brother-in-law.§!

Zhang Banghan
§YBorn:§! §L1885, in Zhenxiong County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §gLong Yun's Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LEntering the Yunnan Higher School in 1905, Zhang Banghan would graduate and then travel to the Nam Ba Vi School in Vietnam. In 1906 he joined the Tongmenghui in Hanoi, and followed Sun Yat-sen travelling within Southeast Asia. Serving as the Chief editor of Singapore's Zhongxing Daily and Hong Kong's China Press, he participated in the Zhennanguan Uprising and the Hekou Uprising, working with Hu Hanmin to shelter revolutionaries. In 1909 he travelled to Europe to study applied science, and returned to China on the orders of Sun Yat-sen.\n\nAppointed director of the department of foreign affairs of the Yunnan Military Government by Cai E, he would leave to obtain an electrochemical engineer certificate in Belgium, returning in 1920 to serve as an engineer at Yaolong Electric Light Factory, director of the Wireless Telegraph Bureau, Instructor at the Aviation School, and Professor of Science and Engineering at Yunnan University.\n\nSupporting Tang Jiyao and Dong Ze in establishing Yunnan University, he would be on the frontlines of negotiations with the Kuomintang as they marched into Guizhou in his capacity as special negotiator of the Department of Foreign Affairs. Recently he has been promoted to serve as Director of the Provincial Construction Department.§!\n\n§YNotes: Distinctly a member of the Yunnan clique with Pro-Kuomintang sympathies.§!
Hu Ruoyu
Heir to a landlord family, Hu Ruoyu was sent to private school when he was young, and later graduated from the local state Secondary School. In 1910 he was admitted to the fourth term of the Yunnan Army Primary School, and participated in the Double Ninth Uprising the following year. In 1912 he left Yunnan for Jiangxi to serve under Li Liejun, and returned to Yunnan on the eve of the Second Revolution. In 1914 he entered the officer class of Kunming Military Academy, and graduated to serve as a squad and battalion commander. During the National Protection War, Hu Ruoyu participated in the battle to retake Gejiu.\n\nOrdered to reinforce the Yunnan Army in Sichuan, he defeated Liu Cunhou, and became a brigade commander in 1918. In 1920 during the Gu Pinzhen coup attempt, Hu led his soldiers in support of Tang Jiyao, but diverted his force to Guangxi to rally with Sun Yat-sen, seizing Guilin from Shen Hongying, and contacting Tang who had fled to Hong Kong. Fighting on behalf of Tang to restore him to power, he led Long Yun and Zhang Ruji to victory, and was in return rewarded as Garrison Commander of Mengzi.\n\nFollowing several blunders from Tang, Hu insisted on attempting a coup against him in 1927. However, Long Yun outmanoeuvred him into instating Tang as puppet governor, with the latter then managing to force both of them back to the negotiating table. With the turn inwards in the 1930s, Hu has strengthened his position in Yunnan's south, relying on Mengzi's connections to German Indochina and extensive mineral wealth.
§YBorn:§! §L1894, in Luoping, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$YUN_hu_ruoyu_bio$§!
§YBorn:§! §L1894, in Luoping, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §PMengzi Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$YUN_hu_ruoyu_bio$§!
Li Xuanting
§YBorn:§! §L1881, in Maguan County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §PMengzi Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LOne of the four previously big garrison commanders under Tang Jiyao, Li Xuanting graduated from the Kunming Military Academy and subsequently participated in the Double Ninth Uprising. Sent to serve in the Yunnan expedition to Sichuan, he would rise to become a battalion commander. In 1914 he returned to Yunnan to serve as a magistrate of Guangnan County and Jianshui County. Fighting in the Huguojun, he would rise to regiment commander.\n\nIn 1918 he became ingratiated directly with the Yunnan Military Governor's Office, and in 1919 served as Chief of Staff of the Second Yunnan Garrison District Headquarters. In 1920 he engaged in skirmishes and conflicts with the Yunnan bandits in the South, and through Gu Pinzhen's coup attempt served as prefect of Mengzi and chief-of-staff of Mengzi regional bandit command. In 1922 he was appointed as garrison commander of Dali for his efforts in returning Tang Jiyao to power, and in 1925 effectively became the warlord of Western Yunnan.\n\nFollowing Tang Jiyao's continued blunders, he conspired with Long Yun, Hu Ruoyu, and Zhang Ruji to depose Tang, but they were outmanoeuvred by the Governor. He then lost his fiefdom of Dali to Sun Du, and was confined to Mengzi. Retained onwards, he serves as commander of the Yunnan Army Military Police Headquarters.§!
Zhang Ruji
§YBorn:§! §L1896, in Qujing County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §PMengzi Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from the Yunnan Army Primary School and Guizhou Army Military Academy, Zhang Ruji served as an assistant in the faculty of the Kunming Military Academy. Participating in the Double Ninth Uprising, he served as the platoon leader of the Yunnan Military Governor's Office, and participated in 1912 in the Yunnan Army's expedition to Guizhou. Entering the Guizhou Army Academy to study, he graduated in 1914 and returned to Yunnan.\n\nIn 1915 he was company commander of Tang Jiyao's personal guard, and fought in the National Protection War against Long Jiguang. In 1921 he continued his service during Gu Pinzhen's tenureship, fighting Shen Hongying in Guangxi. During the Constitutional Protection Headquarters, Zhang served as Commander-in-Chief of the Northern Expedition Advance Army Headquarters, and in 1922 returned to Yunnan under Tang Jiyao.\n\nServing as Commander of the Fourth Army of the Jingguojun, he became commander of the Yunnan Army Military Police Headquarters, and Garrison Commander of Southern Yunnan. In 1923 he was deputy envoy of the Yunnan-Guizhou Allied Forces, and followed Tang Jiyu to occupy Guiyang, serving as Co-Director of the Guizhou Military Reconstruction Supervisory Office. Fighting against Guangxi under the auspices of Tang Jiyao, he then became Garrison Commander of Eastern Yunnan in 1926. The next year, he launched a coup with Hu Ruoyu, Long Yun, and Li Xuanting in an attempt to depose Tang. Since the failure of the coup, he has been confined to Mengzi alongside Li and Hu.§!
Wang Weiyu
§YBorn:§! §L1892, in Mengzi, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §PMengzi Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LWang Weiyu's father ran a small curreny exchange business in Mengzi, and as a result of his entrepreneurial spirit, Wang himself would open a cake and snack stand at the age of 15. Managing to open a mine with a business partner, Li Pin, their enterprise would rapidly grow in size from its founding in 1912 and became moderately successful. However, due to the increase in banditry in the South of Yunnan at the time, their operations were consistently threatened. To this effect, the local mining corporations banded together to form a militia, with Wang selected as their commander.\n\nFounding a series of mining schools and engaged in charity work, Wang is connected to German mining interests in southern Yunnan, and is a local kingpin of the area, even if fairly benevolent. He serves as a minor military officer, and a financial backer, of the Mengzi Clique.§!
Wang Ruwei
§YBorn:§! §L1894, in Kunming, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §PMengzi Clique§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from the Yunnan Military Academy, Wang Ruwei would participate in the Double Ninth Uprising, and afterwards would fight in the National Protection War in Sichuan, serving as a staff officer of Li Xuanting's Army. In 1916 he would be admitted to the 11th Infantry Department of the Kunming Military Academy. After graduation in 1918 he would steadily rise up the ranks of the Yunnan Army, including a stint in Guangdong where he was accused by the Kuomintang government of attempting to aid Chen Jiongming in 1924.\n\nReturning to Yunnan, he developed his own local fiefdom in Maguan in Southeastern Yunnan. By 1927 he was chief of staff of the local Reconstruction Commissioner's office. Accused of recruiting local bandits to help him secure Wenshan and Xichou counties, he would ultimately be unsuccessful in controlling said bandits. When his brother betrayed him, he allegedly murdered him in cold blood, all the while he was solicited by Long Yun and Zhang Ruji to participate in their anti-Tang coup, accepting both of their offers simultaneously. Fleeing upon Long Yun's reported success (albeit short-lived), Wang would find himself within Hu Ruoyu's Mengzi Clique, either out of concern for his safety, or for opportunity.§!\n\n§YNotes: He blurs the line between Military Officer and local bandit.§!
Miao Yuntai
Studying English at the Kunming Dialect School in the late Qing, Miao Yuntai enrolled in a prep class for studying abroad after 1911, and received his tuition to study in the United States in 1913. Studying mining and metallurgy at Southwestern College in Kansas, and later the University of Minnesota, he graduated in 1918 and worked for a steel company in New York. In 1919 he returned to Yunnan, and soon after was appointed by Tang Jiyao as President of he Gejiu Tin Company, which suffered financial losses at the time. Miao managed to clear the debts and adopt modern Western technology, greatly improving the company.\n\nIn 1924 he served as the co-manager of the Yunnan Fudian Bank, and in 1928 he was appoitned as Director of the Provincial Agriculture and Mining Department, alongside a member of the Provincial Assembly. In 1930 he served as Chairman of the Yunnan Provincial Financial Rectification Committee, and in 1931 was the General Manager of the Yunnan Industrial Bank. Continuing to serve as Director of the Industry Department, Miao became general manager of the Yunnan Tin Refining Company, greatly improving the standards of Yunnan tin.\n\nIn 1934 he was appointed president of Fudian New Bank. There, he transformed the provincial financial system from one focused on opium to one focused on tin, and improved the currency of the Yunnan Provincial Banknote. Contributing to the development of local industry, Miao is the chief representative of Yunnan's financial interests.

Xu Zhichen
§YBorn:§! §Lin Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LA key aficionado of the Yunnan provincial adminsitration, Xu Zhichen was appointed in 1916 as Mengzi Supervisor under Cai E, and rising within the ranks under Tang Jiyao, would be appointed Director of the Foreign Affairs Department and of the Finance Department. During the time of the Guangzhou Military Government, Xu Zhichen was sent there to maintain tabs and gather intelligence for Tang Jiyao, and upon his return was retained within the ministry. He is notable for being detested by members of the Yunnan Clique who seem him as a beneficiary of nepotism, or as a core of Tang Jiyao's clique, albeit whether that is truly the case is up to interpretation.§!
Zhang Weihan
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 29th, 1886, in Daguan County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from the Yunnan Public Law and Politics School, in 1908 Zhang Weihan would join the 37th brigade of the 19th Army Regiment, serving as a teacher and secretary. During the Double Ninth Uprising, he participated on the side of the revolutionaries, and joined the Kuomintang in 1912. Serving as a subordinate of Tang Jiyao, he would become head of the General Affairs Section of the Yunnan Provincial Administrative Office, and the local governor of Yanxing and Gejiu counties. In 1915 he would join the Huguojun and fight in the National Protection War agaisnt Long Jiguang.\n\nEntering Sichuan in 1916, he was appointed as the Secretary-General of the Sichuan Military Governor's Office, and returned to Yunnan in 1917. The following year he was sent by Tang to represent Yunnan in the Guangzhou Military Government, and later travelled to Japan to study administration at the Imperial University. in 1922 he returned to Yunnan and was appointed the Governor of Mengzi, and later the first head of the Kunming Municipal Office.\n\nContinuing to serve into the late 1920s and early 1930s, Zhang Weihan would see positions as Director of the Civil Affairs Department and Director of the Foreign Affairs Department. Compliant, skilled, and undoubtedly useful, Weihan is viewed positively by the majority of the Yunnan Clique, except for chiefly Lu Han.§!
Zhou Zhongyue
§YBorn:§! §LOctober 17th, 1876 in Jianchuan County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LOf the Bai nationality, Zhou Zhongyue was born into a scholar's family, and passed the provincial exams in Lijiang and Dali, ranking first in both. In 1900 he graduated from Xiyun Academy in Dali and became a clerk under Zhao Fan, following him to Hunan and Sichuan. Returning 1903 he would once again ace the Provincial Exams, and would travel to Japan to study at government expense, entering Waseda University for Law and Politics. Serving as a bureaucrat in Yunnan Province, he would protest the unequal treaties, and would return to Yunnan in 1911 to serve as Secretary-General to Cai E.\n\nServing in various roles, he would become indispensable to Cai E, the civilian yin to the militaristic yang. When Cai E left Beijing in 1915 to launch the National Protection War, Zhou worked to cover his tracks, and travelled to Japan for his own safety. Travelling to Sichuan in 1916, he would serve as Luo Peijin's Secretary-General, and then in the same capacity for Tang Jiyao. In 1919 he was acting governor of Yunnan Province, and later official governor. In 1922 he was Yunnan Salt Transport Commissioner under Tang Jiyao, Privy Councilor, and Chairman of the Provincial Legal Committee.\n\nIn 1926 he became director of the Yunnan Provincial Internal Affairs Department, and briefly mediating inner-Yunnan Clique politics, he would retire. In 1930 he became Director of the Yunnan General History Museum, and compiled provincial history. Recalled in 1934 by Tang Jiyao, he serves as the Yunnanese Civil Affairs Minister.§!
Li Wenyi
§YBorn:§! §L1903, in Wuhan, Hubei Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from Hubei Provincial Women's Normal School in 1924, Li Wenyi would join the Chinese Syndicalist Party and the Kuomintang in March 1926, serving in the underground Hubei Provincial Committee of the Kuomintang. Especially engaged with the women's liberation movement, she organised and led the work of the Hubei Provincial Women's Association. Member of the Women's Committee of the Chinese Syndicalist Party, she would leave the country in 1928 to study at Sun Yat-sen University in France, and would return to serve as a member of the Shanghai Hudong District Committee of the Chinese Syndicalist Party in the 1930s.\n\nSlowly becoming enwrapped with Third Way politics following the Shanghai Uprising, she would informally associate herself with Zhang Junmai's National Renaissance Society, the Crescent Moon Society, Rural Reconstruction Movement, Political Science Clique, and all other Third Way parties or associations. A leading Feminist in the Chinese women's struggle for equality, she moved to Kunming to establish a provincial women's organisation there, based off of her work in Hubei.§!
Lu Daoyuan
§YBorn:§! §L1900, in Changning County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LGraduating from the 13th Infantry Department of the Kunming Military Academy, Lu Daoyuan would serve in the Fourth Guard Regiment of the Jingguojun from 1922 to 1927. Promoted to Battalion commander, he would circumnavigate Yunnan Clique politics to position himself as a Pro-Long Yun Pro-Tang Jiyao member of the clique. Opposed to Zhang Ruji, and Hu Ruoyu's ambitions. Promoted to commander of the Fourth Guard Regiment in 1928, he would become a brigadier general that same year.\n\nEngaging in multiple skirmishes through the late 1920s, Lu has exhibited extraordinary wit and bravery in light of severe challenges in the Yunnan Army, and is noted for his severe propensity to steel-willed determination in light of overwhelming odds.§!
Liao Xingchao
Writing poems under his uncle's stewardship, Liao Xingchao graduated from the Kunming Junior Normal School in 1911. Enwrapped in the Xinhai revolution, he would abandon a career in literature and join the military, serving in a student volunteer dare-to-die squad. Admitted to the forth infantry class of the Yunnan Military Academy, he became classmates and subsequently close friends with Long Yun. Graduating in 1914, he was assigned to serve under Zhu De. Fighting in the National Protection War, he was promoted at its conclusion for his meritorious service.\n\nServing as chief of staff of Jin Handing in 1918, he stayed on the sidelines during Gu Pinzhen's coup in 1920. Under Yang Ruxuan, he then fought in the Yunnan Army in Guangdong against Chen Jiongming, in 1922. In 1924 he he became a sworn brother of Liao Zhongkai, but later that same year would leave China to study in France, entering the history department of Lyon University. Meeting there the future founders of the Young China Party, Zeng Qi and Li Huang, he became affiliated with their young ideology.\n\nReturning to China in 1925, he had to flee to Vietnam following oppression from Chiang Kai-shek, and then travelled to Japan to study in 1927. Returning to the country in 1935, he was appointed as Long Yun's Chief of Staff, although he is not particularly attached to his clique.

§YBorn:§! §LFebruary 12th, 1895 in Huaning County, Yunnan Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §nYoung China Party§!\n§YBackground:§! §L$YUN_liao_xingchao_bio$§!\n\n§YNotes: He is a close friend of Long Yun, and of Jin Handing.\nConsidered to be the leader of the Yunnan Provincial YCP branch.§!
Liu Xianqian
§YBorn:§! §LDecember 28th, 1864 in Xingyi County, Guizhou Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LIndependent§!\n§YBackground:§! §LCousin of Liu Xianshi, Liu Xianqian was admitted to a county school as the son of a bureaucratic family, but after a horrible string of academic failures, he instead idled at home. In 1902 he served in a local militia to repel Guangxi bandits, and was promoted to the rank of captain by the Qing. In 1911 he was appointed as commander of the Guangxi Frontier Defence Army by Governor Shen Bingkun, and fought the revolutionaries against the Qing, actively working with his cousin to do so.\n\nPromoted during Tang Jiyao's takeover of Guizhou, he became a local warlord, and rose through the ranks until 1915. With the National Protection War in full swing, Liu Xianqian would would reluctantly fight on the side of the NPA. Embroiled in the Guizhou factional struggle of the late 1910s, Xianqian followed his brother to Yunnan after the Minjiu Incident. Returning in 1922 alongside his cousin and Yuan Zuming, the Lius would be betrayed by Yuan, and so would side with Tang Jiyao for their reinstatement.\n\nOusting Yuan with Tang Jiyu in 1923, Xianqian would become acting governor in 1924. Aiding Tang Jiyao in invading Liangguang in 1925, his forces would be blunted by the New Guangxi Clique, and his homebase would be reinvaded by Yuan Zuming. Announcing his resignation, Liu retired to Xingyi, and following the chaos in the Southwest and the manouvring of the Kuomintang into Guizhou, was recalled into action by Tang Jiyao.§!
Liu Peiquan
§YBorn:§! §L1893, in Nanhai County, Guangdong Province, Great Qing§!\n§YAlignment:§! §LNeutral§!\n§YBackground:§! §LApplying to the Zengbu Vocational School and accepted in 1910, Liu Peiquan befriended there Chen Ce and Zhang Fakui - with future observations being made that the three individuals represented the Navy, Army, and Air Force of China respectively. In 1915, Liu worked as a reporter for Juehun Daily, reporting on the flying performances of Chinese-American pilot Tan Gen, and he subsequently became his secretary. In 1920, he was hired by Tang Jiyao as Director of the new Yunnan Aviation Department, and to serve as principal of the aviation school and senior councilor of the Yunnan Army Headquarters.\n\nIn 1922 he recruited a group of aviation personnel from Guangdong to Kunming, ordered six aircraft from Hong Kong, and set up the foundations for the establishment of the Yunnan Air Force. In 1926, he was briefly appointed as the Air Force Commander of the Eastern Route Army of the National Revolutionary Army, but would soon defect back to the Yunnan Clique following NRA failures on the field. In 1930, he served in the Preparatory Office of China Airlines, and then organised the Southwest Civil Aviation Company in 1933.§!

Autonomous Armies
For all of his posturing, rallying, and shaking of fists, Tang Jiyao's hold on Yunnan's ever ferocious militarism is fragile, and brittle. Deposed by Gu Pinzhen in the early 1920s, Tang Jiyao's influence never recovered, and control of his armies slowly slipped out of his grasp. Increasingly reliant on bandit armies, and increasingly dejected by his closest commanders such as Long Yun, or Hu Ruoyu, the state of the once proud Yunnan Army is that of a sorry one. Local garrison commanders and generals alike run their fractured units like fiefdoms based on personal loyalties, no longer swearing true fealty to the provincial governor. Run amok, the King of the Southwest is nothing more than the King of Kunming, and even that is debatable.

Legacy of the Huguojun
The Huguojun, or National Protection Army, were the great saviours of the Republic in its time of great struggle against Imperial Ambitions. The perfidious Yuan Shikai, sought to tear up the hard fought values of the Xinhai Revolution, trample over the constitution, and declare himself as the Hongxian Emperor. Yet the brave leaders of Yunnan stood defiant against his desire for autocracy. Liang Qichao, Cai E, Tang Jiyao, Li Liejun, Yang Sen, all of these brave men are heroes of modern China, having defied the odds against the Central Government's army, triumphing against the likes of Wu Peifu, Cao Kun and Feng Yuxiang.\n\nWithout a doubt, their brave victories could not have been so swift, so hard fought, if it was not for the superior training, vigour, and dedication of the graduates of Kunming Military Academy. Once again a time of great conflict is upon the nation, and the leadership of Yunnan must again march to save the nation. Recalling the glory days of the Huguojun, the Yunnan Army is focused on excellent drill, officers and command, rather than superior armaments. For if one thing is for certain, a devout militaristic warrior spirit can triumph even the most well equipped division.

For too long have the Burmese held onto rightful Chinese land, granted due to the weakness of the decadent Qing. If we are to be the true protectors of China, we must unite all of it, no matter how small. The Yellow Book Affair, as it has become to be known, is perhaps scoffed at by foreign powers, but for us Chinese, it is merely setting things right.
Officer's Prerogative
Emphasising the initiative and command capacity of lower level commanders within the Yunnan Army, the military high command has granted them the ability to discern the best course of action to achieve their tactical military goals in line with the overall strategic goal. Our officers are our strongest asset, and it would be wise to trust their judgement.
People's Arms Manufacturing
Evidently, the Chinese people rush to support our armies every step of the way, however our men still lack guns and armaments. If we are to truly stand up as the defenders of the people and their rights, we must mobilise them not just in the fields but the factories also. For every bullet made, is another oppressor of the people's will vanquished.
Frontlines, Everywhere
It is not just enough for potential invaders to have to engage our military forces in direct combat, for they must also deal with the mobilisation of guerilla units in their backlines. The deeper they march into our territory, the increasingly fragile their supply lines become, and the bolder our guerillas become. Continuously, we will wage a complete and utter people's war against invaders.
Yunnanese Auftragstaktik
Auftragstaktik, or mission-type tactics, is a method of command practised by delegating junior military leaders a clearly-defined strategic objective, timeframe, and the necessary forces to complete said objective, whilst leaving the execution and best application of resources provided to their diligence. With our military theorists having studied the Weltkrieg, the superior performance of the German Imperial Army was in-part due to their delegation of command. To replicate their successes, we shall employ our own level of lower officer autonomy.
Hundred Regiments Offensive
A campaign masterminded by our greatest military theorists and strategists, the military high command has drafted an ambitious and extensive plan requiring only one hundred regiments. With one hundred regiments, our army can annihilate any invader or opponent, no matter their strength. Launching forward the great might and spirit of the Chinese nation, our enemies are sure to cower in utter terror.

The Republic of China Navy
The precursor to a modern Chinese republican navy dates back to the early days of the Xinhai Revolution when the Ministry of the Navy was formed in the Provisional Government of the Republic of China. The former Qing Dynasty admiral Liu Guanxiong became the first Minister of Navy during this turbulent period. Following the Warlord Era and the subsequent Xuantong Restoration, many elements of the Republic of China Navy fled south for the Kuomintang and Federalist strongholds, while many others chose to remain loyal to the government in Beijing. With the Chinese country finally nominally unified under one banner, the time has come once more to revitalise and rebuild the Republic of China Navy so that it is truly worthy of protecting China's great maritime interests.

The modern Chinese Federalist rose and fell across the first half of the 1920s. A coalition of constitutionalists, democrats, and regionalists - they banded together amid the continued North-South stalemate at the peak of the warlord era. Building Chinese Federalism

Bolstered further by Tang Jiyao's penchant to favour the intellectual class, and by the welcoming of various groups fleeing repression such as the Crescent Moon Society, it has truly become a civil utopia. As a model to be emulated, provincial efforts first seen in Yunnan have been mandated all across the various provinces. With a government staffed by the spiritual successors of scholar-officials, common illiteracy will be but a figment of the past come a generation or two.
The Anti-Partisan Civil Service
Bureaucratic malaise has often been the bane of many successive Chinese Governments. Incompetence, corruption, favouritism, Xinhai both unleashed egalitarianism unto the masses, and simultaneously selfishness unto the once lauded scholar-official classes. Few have resisted the blatant patronage of the previous Central Governments, and those that have often found themselves isolated from true arsenals of power, except perhaps the Diplomatic Corps. Nonetheless, an anti-partisan Civil Service should be the model for which the nation strives towards. As pioneered in its best light in America, and championed by Washington and Jefferson, the bureaucrats must be of a patriotic calibre, with no affiliation towards any political parties. Officials must, above all else, prioritise the country, not politics.
Declaration of Total War
This war is a total war! A war against despotism! A war against tyranny! A war against those that would plunge China into the all-consuming void of autocracy! Yunnan cannot sit idly by as the Republic she has fought for tears itself apart further and further. There can be no recompense for cutthroats and no mercy for traitors. Yunnan has been the hotbed of professional soldiery since the beginning of the century, animated by sense of duty, every Yunnanese soldier is disciplined, obedient, and willing to sacrifice to preserve national integrity.
The Legacy of Decades of Revolt
Yunnan is by far the most well-poised province to ensure that the legacy of Xinhai need not perish here. The elected President should be expected to create that of a shared nation-state, and yet nothing but chaos has been wrought in his wake. There is no ensuring of survival, but only the undermining of the political institutions of the Republic and abuse of powers for personal advantage. Repeating the machinations of Yuan Shikai may fool others, but the Yunnanese know better, and it is our destiny passed down through decades to construct national unity through the emancipation of bloodshed.
Before Lights Out
Freedom! Freedom is what we fight for! To retake China's own confidence and position on the world stage! There can be no failure on the road to our triumphant victory! With our backs against the wall, we must but strike out relentlessly, burning it all in our wake to secure the return of the Middle-Kingdom to stability and harmony, not tyranny or despotism.
Dead Men Walking
Were we not worthy? Our ranks have broken. Our morale is shattered. Our men no longer have the will to fight... If China is truly to be sent plummeting into inevitable dictatorship, we can only hope that history vindicates our struggle...
All Power to the Constitutional Government
The Federalist project cannot be one of a lone titan alone, no. It is the mission of the emancipation of every province and every citizen of China, and as such requires cross-cooperation between all willing allies. As a sign of this, the Yunnan Provincial Government has begun to forward industrial resources and materiel towards the leading province of the Federalist movement, in order to strengthen their combined alliance.
Empowering Local Assemblies
It is the duty of the Local Self-Government movement to ensure that the Chinese people are ready and willing to participate in true civic democracy. As a result, it is necessary for the various Federalist provincial movements to, at least as far as we can see, to empower and experiment with democracy at a local level. Provincial and national constitutional rule can surely wait, firstly the people must be familiar with governance in their own way.
Paying the Cost for Peace
Ever the uneasy gun at our back, the Mengzi and Long Yun Cliques of the Yunnan Clique have long held themselves up to an uneasy and tense peace with the King in Kunming. With a focus on insular policy, it is inevitable that the disloyal Defence Commissioners will have to be brought to heel, and forever a savvy politician, the Governor knows that no general can resist the cannonshot of 20,000 silver dollars. It might be costly now, but it will secure our fortunes in the future.
Pursuit of the Insular Policy
With the geopolitical situation within China enabling an insular policy of reconstruction and conciliation in the wake of an extra buffer on the provincial borders, Miao Yuntai has been thrust into action to pursue all and any of his economic policy reforms. Effectively retreating from active military attempts to impose unity on China, at least when possible, Yunnan will become a metropolis in due time.
Support for Patriotic Labour Unions
It is only natural that the workers receive their due representation in Chinese society. They are but a true, earnest, honest, and sincere part of China's grand industrialisation. However, they cannot be led astray by revolutionaries who would seek to abuse them for their own ends, nor can they let their role in society become politicised in of itself. Apolitical patriotism is the virtue we should all aspire towards, and it is only right that the workers see to that notion too.
Expansion of the Fudian Bank
Ever since the gradual reduction in central authority due to the decline of the Qing, foreign banknotes had partially superceded native Chinese currency in border provinces. The Promotion of Education to the Masses
The true wellspring of China's inequality and misfortune is not entirely that of the plight of the peasantry, but that of their literacy. Of their education. The bedrock of any and all modern developed society is that of a standard level of education maintained and assured by the state. When hardly any citizen can read, how can they vote? As the new intellectual cornucopia of the nation, Yunnan must lead the charge in advancing national literacy efforts.

The reality is much more nuanced. Many warlords were well-educated and kept up to date with international political trends, and while most had a ruthless streak, that was hardly uncommon in China. The popular image of a warlord is that of an arbitrary tyrant: a cruel, uneducated autocrat with little regard to human life, ceaseless avarice, and surrounded by a decadent court of vice and yesmen. The reality is much more nuanced. Many warlords were well-educated and kept up to date with international political trends, and while most had a ruthless streak, that was hardly uncommon in China. Furthermore, while most were autocrats, they hardly ruled alone: they were backed by a complex web of politicians, generals, economic elites, social figures, and intellectuals.\n\nNo strangers to such a state of affairs, the Yunnan Clique has finally sprung itself upon its lessers and the utopians. They have fought and bled for China, and know all too well the dire consequences of idealism in this cruel world. Only the strong can survive, not those with their heads in the clouds.

Celebrating Revolutionary Tradition
Modern China is a nation born within the flames of revolutionary fervour, there can be no denying it. It is a nation of sacrifices, of courage, and as such it is only natural that government's embrace, celebrate, and extol such virtues. In this all consuming civil war, you cannot deny the righteous Chinese youth and citizens their right to exhibit their anger at the cruel state of the nation's affairs, instead you must harness it to see a new China form from the ashes.
Hinging Everything on Total Victory
Ever in pursuit of that decisive victory, that first kick in the door that shall cause the obliteration of the enemy's lines and morale, this is what the Kunming Military Academy trained the Yunnan Army for. Vigour, the endurance of hardship, and the upholding of a revolutionary republican ideal. Only through such difficulties, may the nation exhibit reform and progress.
China's Praetorians
Defending China's republican institutions, the constitution, and her frontier border with their lives, the Yunnan Army is the last line of defence for many Chinese. Called upon once more to fight for the values often forgotten by those in the Capital, the Yunnanese shall not allow autocracy, or the flagrant abuse of law and judiciary to see the sundering of Five Races Under One Union.
Faltering Hopes
And to what end? To what end did so many brave soldiers die? Without order, or without reason, it would appear that the morale of the Yunnanese lines have utterly collapsed, welcoming mass defections, surrendering of key positions, and an ultimate sense of defeatism within the ranks. Whilst the high command insists to the Governor that they can push on, few share their optimism.
Increasing Isolationist Politics
To combat increasing hostilities outside of the sphere of Yunnan's control, the provincial administration has adopted a policy of isolationism, outwardly stating their abandoning of national ambitions, hoping to retain their corner of the Southwest alone. In conjunction with this reorientation, the Governor has ordered an increase in the development of fortifications and trenches at key weakpoints within Yunnan and Guizhou.
Culling Incompetents
Many within the Yunnan Army still retain loyalties to Tang Jiyao, or other spectres of the past administration, and without a stable control on the military arm, few can expect to survive years in office as Governor of any province, let alone one as militarised as Yunnan. By conducting a swift and thorough army purge, reorganising and slimming divisions, alongside promoting key loyalists, the Yunnan Army is undergoing a stringent period of reconstruction.
Continuation of the Tang Policies
Whilst Governor Tang may no longer hold the reigns in his steadfast grip, much of his old administration has managed to survive both the recent coup and turmoil before that. Indispensable in their efforts to keep the provincial bureaucracy from falling to anarchy, the new Governor has deigned it a pragmatic yet unpopular move to retain much of the existing civil servants, whilst continuing the reform plans implemented and produced by Tang Jiyao's administration.
Tribute for Autonomy
Whilst not wholly conquered, the Yunnan Clique has at least nominally sworn fealty to another Chinese warlord with far more national ambitions. In order to preserve local autonomy, and to prevent another disastrous offensive war, the Yunnan provincial administrations has begun to forward materiel and industrial aid to their allies, as a sort of tithe.
Maintaining Open Mindedness
Ever the pragmatist, Long Yun has often kept his options ever open and ever available in matters involving Yunnan's external positioning. Be it Reformist, Warlord, or Chairman, Long has shaken hands with associates of all backgrounds, and cast himself equally as a democrat, a principled Governor, or resilient revolutionary. But at home, make no mistake, the King of Yunnan rules with a harsh policy, annihilating any potentially destabilising force foolish enough to rear its head.
Revival of Export Militarism
Foolishly, previous Yunnanese administrations sought to retreat entirely from their duties and investments outside of provincial control. Opting to abandon national ambitions and isolating the Clique from the rest of China, they bore witness to a great diminishing of Yunnan's capacity to exert itself. Not only within the nation, but on the borders in the West, and Southeast. Yunnan is a province known for being the kingmaker of the Southwest, and it is time that it acts like it.
The Yunnan Provincial Economic Committee
Founded by Lu Chongren, Miao Yuntai, Li Peiyan, Zhang Banghan, and many others, the Yunnan Provincial Economic Committee's purpose is to rectify the dire economic situation that the province has recently found itself in. With an increased period of warfare and a collapse in financial stability alongside trade, the YPEC has been tasked with finding novel solutions to Yunnan's current difficulties. Through methods such as the reformation of the tax services, establishment of new bureaus and collection networks alongside the levying of new taxes, monopolies on opium and other goods, and nationalisation of provincial banking services, the YPEC has been granted a relatively free hand to conduct itself as it sees fit to save Yunnan's economy.

Establishing a Provincial Monopoly
Devised by Lu Chongren, the Yunnan's new economic model revolves around the concept of finance sustaining production, which there onwards enriches finance. By expanding state-owned enterprises and vigorously improving local production methods whilst enforcing a strict state monopoly, Lu hopes to create an infinitely sustainable economic growth model. Competing with Miao Yuntai, Lu's Provincial Enterprise Bureau and four major banks have become the bedrock of his ambitious economic reforms.
Architect of a New China
Perhaps one of the greatest economists to have offered his services during the Warlord Era, and having served every Yunnanese provincial administration, Miao Yuntai rose to fame initially after his tenure as general manager of the Yunnan Tin Company saw the drastic rise in profits of the local tin industry, and as president of the Fudian Bank, alongside the provincial Chamber of Commerce. The Architect of much of Yunnan's economic success, he has once again managed to secure a lucrative economic deal with various foreign contractors to Yunnan's great benefit.
Industrialisation by the Train Track
Whilst railways may have led to the collapse of the Zhili Clique, it is an ever present fact that the interior of China still remains relatively untouched by modern track. With the Kunming-Haiphong Railway being the largest arterie in and out of Kunming, it serves almost exclusively to export goods such as tin and opium to Indochina, and onto further markets. If China is truly to be a modern nation, it must equally have plentiful modern track to sustain its industrialisation.
Devastating Economic Catastrophe
With the pursuit of unification of the nation, China has underwent a myriad of terrible troubles along such an unsteady path. Perhaps the worst of them all however, is that of the economic consequences of decades of warfare and warlordism. Hyperinflation, rampant militarism, civil war, banditry, and a general lack of financial stability or cohesion amongst the provinces has resulted in the mass withdrawal of investors, the general inability to efficiently collect taxes, or for there to be a unified national currency or tax system in the first place. AS the nation recovers, it is imperative that its best minds work swiftly to repair the presently dire economic straits.
Inexperience and Lack of Cohesion
Politically, the Dian Clique is far from a unified outfit. Appealing to all manner of local politicians, officers, and bureaucrats, the only ruling ideology of the day was inherent republican pragmatism. Similarly, Yunnan provincial political dynamics would ensure that the deciding fact of one's own success in the province was commonly that of one's own strength. Yet having emerged to rule the national stage, matters have become incredibly complex, requiring a certain level of skillful tact to navigate, all the while various organised political outfits besiege the Central Government for some sort of agenda.
The State Within the State
As the heirs to a legacy of Chinese militarism and the foundations of modern warlordism, it should become no surprise that the inherent vices of the Dian Clique have re-emerged with their new successes and conquests. Requiring the administration of vast swathes of territory far outside their home province, the National Army has become something of a hyper militarised and over-inflated goliath. Not only enshrining the values of the administration within the youth, but preparing them for their future as soldiers and contributing citizens to the Chinese nation-state, the Youth League informs them of their responsibilities to the national whole, and of their obligations towards the government and army. With personal politics remaining one's own sphere, the National Youth League Affairs Bureau strives for apolitical training, and influence within the youth, without the trappings of similar initiatives from the Kuomintang, or Young China Party.
Obligations of the Military
It is only natural that the warriors of unification, national self-strengthening, and the Republican revolution be the same men to lead the Chinese nation-state onto a new era of prosperity. Obligated to defend China's interests at home and abroad, the professional soldier should conduct himself in national politics akin to that of their career in the common soldiery. Whilst it is preferable that the men of words remain in their civilian spheres, and the men of guns in their military spheres, should the nation be at risk of subversion or otherwise, officers must but only seize the initiative to correct China's course.

Responsibility for the Nation-State
The President of China is expected in his role to serve in the creation of a shared nation-state, in which every citizen could assume their respective responsibility for the well-being of the nation state, and its survival. As such, the President's priorities must always align with constitutionalism and national unity, not partisan politics or other loyalties. Under a strong president, and a strongly centralised republic, China should be guaranteed stability, progress, and harmony. And should any such facet fail or be disregarded, the state's veto above all else is that of its Praetorian Guard, the armed forces.
Economic Regulation Committee
Consolidated as an outgrowth of local provincial efforts to modernise the Yunnan tax systems and reduce inefficiencies, the now national Economic Regulation Committee is a multi-faceted governmental organisation tasked with a myriad of duties to rectify state finances, incentivise investments, and provide for the nation's people. Abolishing all previous loans and levies, streamlining currencies into that of a new resilient paper currency and affirming taxes to the rate of five paper dollars for every existing silver dollar, and applying extra taxes on luxury goods are but a few of the many tasks the committee is involved with.\n\nIn conjunction, it serves to enhance and strengthen the state monopoly on various key nationalised industries, the advancement of local and international investments, repaying of foreign debts, and the further industrialisation of China wholesale.

Maintaining the Paradigm
Strained to the breaking point, Yunnan's economy has long been in disrepair. Already weak and unstable from militarisation efforts, Black Monday has brought the house of cards falling. The clock is ticking: We must find a way to stave off total collapse with the limited resources at Yunnan's disposal lest our ambitions be nipped in the bud by permanent economic collapse.
Alleviate the Gentry
The gentry have played a pivotal role in Yunnan's history, and were of course badly hit by the current economic crisis. While Cai E directed Yunnan province towards a more martial and militaristic direction, Governor Tang Jiyao has attempted to somewhat tune down the militarisation. With the economy in tatters, the economy needs to be tended to once more, starting with the gentry.
Expanding Tin Exports
Unemployment is another major issue owing to the current economic crisis. Fortunately, There is a way to combat it by using Yunnan's historically rich tin mines to our advantage. By expanding the mines, we will produce far more jobs for the unemployed, as well as gaining the ability to further exploit Yunnan's rich tin reserves for expanded exports.
Dual Development
While Yunnan's industrial capabilities remain lacklustre, the province is rich with natural resources. While traditionally an export economy, Yunnan's resources can be used to develop domestic light industries and diversify the economy. Resources not shipped to Western markets shall be used to develop burgeoning light industries in Yunnan's cities.
Nationalisation of Critical Industries
The current economic crisis cannot be left to the whims of the free market. No, the provincial administration will require a more hands on approach, one which requires more revenue to be accumulated in the governorship. On top of that, nationalisation will allow the state to create new jobs for the unemployed, prevent labour abuse at the hands of the gentry, and prepare the province for war.
Appealing to Berlin
Yunnan's main connection to the international market is via the French-constructed Kunming Haiphong Rail and it acts as the province's greatest weapon supplier. The Germans have since inherited the rail, but have not continued military sales to the same degree. To incentivise Berlin, we will of course promise to keep our Vietnamese workers in check and implement stricter anti-banditry measures...
Centralising Taxation
Inefficient taxation has long been a major issue for Yunnan's economy. However, attempts to centralise and streamline it have failed due to corrupt officials and a lack of personnel. With a need for increased provincial revenue, the province must centralise tax collection under one ministry of finance, rather than having it handled by multiple independent bureaus.
Kunming to Guiyang
Ever since the Yuan Dynasty brought the Southwest into China, Yunnan and Guizhou have shared a common destiny, bound together by circumstance and geography. Indeed, the two provinces suffered together during the last years of the Qing Dynasty. Sadly, the Northern Expedition split the two, with Kunming under Tang Jiyao, and Guizhou under the KMT. However, Dian and Qian once again fly the same flag, and never shall the two be split again!
United Xinan Model
While Yunnan and Guizhou finally have once again become one, much work remains to be done. The Chinese Southwest has long been one of the least developed regions of China, and economic reforms thus far have been less than fruitful. If China is to escape the Century of Humiliation, the provinces must first liberate themselves from backwardness and foreign domination, and Yunnan and Guizhou will lead the charge.
Develop Yunnan's Smelting Company
Tang Jiyao's ambitions were not only to tackle industrialisation, but mining as well. One of the core pillars of the Yunnanese economy, Tang attempted to oversee the breaking of foreign dominance in mining, to limited success. Another critical avenue of economic modernisation came in the form of smelteries, and if Yunnan is to become a modern province, Yunnan's smelting must be done by Yunnanese companies in parallel to Yunnanese mining.
Provincial Reconstruction
Continuously devastated by never ending crises, Yunnan province remains crippled by poverty, banditry, and the scars of militarisation. Yunnanese governors have traditionally been successful in curbing banditry in the short term, but only the economic recovery will truly bring Yunnanese banditry down for good. Hand in hand, the gentry, peasantry, and government will work as one to bring much needed relief to Yunnan province.
Financial Regulation
Yunnan has long had a patchwork of tax collection, dating back to the Qing Dynasty. The last Viceroy of the Yun-Gui region, Li Jingxi, attempted to streamline taxation, but was unable to accomplish much before the Xinhai Revolution occurred. Even 20 years after Li Jingxi, Yunnan's problems remain much the same, and the system of taxation is no different. Governor [ROOT.GetLeader] must finally tackle proper taxation and finance if Yunnan is to prosper.
Centralised Planning
Leaving the economy to the whims of the market leaves it open to foreign domination, a fate the Chinese people know all too well. Modernity must be fostered with a firm hand from the provincial government, which will shepard the economy to a respectable condition and see Yunnan and Guizhou work as a well-oiled machine and achieve common prosperity for the peoples of both provinces.
Cultivating the Bureaucratic Apparatus
The state cannot hope to regulate and manage the economy efficiently or effectively without a proper bureaucracy. While the gentry would prefer that the government not stick its hands into their affairs, the government must do exactly that, for gentry while a respectable class in Yunnan are also an obstacle to our agenda. The Yunnan Economic Commission will help oversee Yunnan's smelting, mining modernisation, and agricultural development with gentry support.
Militarising Guizhou's Ironwork Industry
Yunnan and Guizhou, despite being unified under a single flag, still fall behind their provincial neighbours. Yunnanese militarisation is the only feasible option for the peoples of Dian and Qian to gain a leg up against the threats to our North and East. Guizhou still remains less militarised when compared to Yunnan, and its resources must be marshalled by Kunming in order to defend them from common threats.
Promoting Self Sufficiency
The imperialists that we are forced to serve enjoy their luxuries in their western cities whilst the Chinese people are chained to a life of poverty as we produce lucrative and essential materials for their consumption. The disease of capitalism ravages our lands and forces all to play, but wherever possible, the government of [ROOT.GetLeader] will keep the West at arm's length. Death to Imperialist extortion, long live the eternal spirit of the Chinese nation!
Grand Southern Reconstruction Project
Yunnan has known nothing but strife since the Century of Humiliation began, but the administration's reformist aspirations have culminated in the form of the Grand Southern Reconstruction Project, the most ambitious economic reform agenda which will propel not only Yunnan, but southern China and soon the entire nation into the modern age! Long live [ROOT.GetLeader]!
Streamline Superfluous Taxes
During the Qing Dynasty, Yunnanese taxation bureaus were established independent of each other, causing great headaches for the Kunming Administration. While the late Qing reforms saw attempts to centralise taxation, gentry opposition ultimately prevented total centralisation, and Yunnan became engulfed in war during the Republican era. At long last, plans have finally been laid out to restructure Yunnanese taxation.
Resurgent South
Day after day, factories come to life in Kunming. Yunnanese exports line the pockets of the Yunnanese people instead of Western powers. Ever since the Qing Dynasty, Southern China has seen the economy stagnate due to neglect, but the Grand Southern Reconstruction Project has seen long awaited development finally arrive. Let it be known that the south rises like a phoenix and the Chinese Century is at hand!
Waking the Tiger
Ever since Cai E took the reins of Yunnanese governance, he has had to balance his militaristic ambitions with his reformist outlook, but ultimately chose to militarise the province heavily. Tang Jiyao aimed to rebalance the economy, but also couldn't abandon militarism. With the government putting all focus into economic reform, the state now finds itself at a crossroad, between continued militarisation or full rebalancing.
National Defence Economy
Yunnan finds itself encircled by vultures on all sides. Even with the wealth of Guangzhou at hand, the state must be prepared to push the Chinese worker, merchant and soldier to work as one. A strong emphasis on discipline will encourage everyone to work with sweat, blood and tears to bring about the national unification that will bring about a reborn nation. Without the army, there would be no new China!
Movement for Guangxi's Reconstruction
Like Yunnan, Guangxi is the site of a decades-old militarist programme. During the short rule of Li Zongren and his New Guangxi Clique, the Guangxi Reconstruction Movement was adopted to modernise the province whilst keeping to Lu Rongting's militarist principles. While these reforms lack the scale of Yunnan's, supporting Guangxi's reconstruction will fuel the Southern war machine.
Urban Self Initiatives
During the late Qing reforms, Yunnanese Viceroy Li Jingxi attempted an ambitious programme of bureaucratic and economic reform. Ultimately gentry opposition limited his reforms to just the big cities of the province. While today's administration seeks out reform across all parts of China's economy and society in a centralised manner, limited autonomy to urban centres may churn out the reforms of tomorrow.
Dictating Foreign Trade
Over the years, foreign businessmen from Japan and the West have been exploiting our resources and killing the livelihoods of Chinese producers. No longer shall this be the case as we shall enact protectionist policies that will enrich the hands of the people rather than the pockets of those who have exploited us for centuries. The west found itself kept at arm's length under the Cantonese system, and so it shall be again.
Merchants Under the State
Under earlier Guangdong administrations, the Merchant Corps enjoyed unprecedented levels of freedom, not unlike the Yunnanese Gentry. Taking advantage of the government's hospitality, the Merchant Corps has stolen from the Chinese people and acted as tax collectors for the Western powers and Tokyo. No longer will the Merchant Corps enjoy autonomy, for the nation must be united in a singular, patriotic direction.
Industrial Self Reliance
While Eastern China profits greatly from foreign investment and foreign-built factories, Yunnan's geography and location mandated that a more autarkic policy be adopted, lest the province become a resource state for the Great Powers, as it had been in previous decades. Adopting a more autarkic, self-reliant, or even mercantilist outlook on industrial modernisation will keep the south reliant on strength that is their own.
Expanding the Kunming Arsenal
Kunming cannot always rely on the mountains that surround it, so it must be ready to defend herself at all times and that means it needs a lot of equipment to be able to survive. Investing into the construction of new factories will help the efforts of building not only a stronger lifeline for the city of Kunming but to the rest of Yunnan and beyond. The Kunming Arsenal will surpass the Hankou, Taiyuan, and Shenyang Arsenals!
Union of State and Industry
In the modern age, to modernise the military requires the modernisation of the economy. To modernise the economy, the bureaucratic apparatuses of the state must be operating at peak efficiency. The great effort of modernising the south requires all hands on deck, and total cooperation between all sectors of the economy and all aspects of the government. Yunnan must become a fierce machine of war and modernity.
The South Unshackled
Since the fall of the Ming Dynasty, Southern China has known nothing but despair and decay. Heightened by the Century of Humiliation, patriotic Chinese could only watch as the one-time centre of the world was pillaged by cruel Europeans and Japanese traitors. Southern China's time in the gutter has ended now, and the Guangzhou Government marches forward, modernised, rejuvenated, and free!
Southern Reconstruction Commission
While [ROOT.GetLeader]'s administration finds itself in a precarious position, it also offers a unique opportunity for Chinese renewal. Yunnan's recent history has been rife with militarism, but now with militarist institutions at a low, rebalancing Yunnan's economy is finally feasible. If China is to prosper, it must recover and be given breathing room. To that end, the Southern Reconstruction Commission will be the first major step away from militarism and towards prosperity.
Promoting National Light Industry
The century of humiliation has deeply damaged China's light industry at the national level, furthering the people's suffering. No longer! Significant modernisations, developments and reforms will ensure that the nation's light industry finally prospers like it was always meant to, and lifts Chinese people up from their suffering under imperialism.
Promoting Foreign Investment
Guangzhou's importance not just to the South, but to China itself, cannot be overstated. During the Qing Dynasty, the Cantonese System helped control western trade in China, and give Beijing a preferential trade imbalance with the West. This system was abolished after the Opium Wars, but its legacy rings on. If China is to rise to prosperity, western trade must be encouraged, though not to the detriment of Chinese businesses. Both China and the West must profit from this arrangement.
Friendly But Cautious
While foreign investment is undoubtedly necessary to further national economic development, such an effort also demands carefulness in order to not fall into the trap of overreliance to foreign powers and their imperialism once more. In the Northeast, Marshal Zhang Zuolin finds himself hopelessly reliant on Tokyo, and it serves as a cautionary tale for the rest of China. China may greet the West with a firm handshake and a friendly smile but always remain on guard.
Developing the Middle Man
To make sure that foreign investments in Guangzhou go all according to plan, and maximise their efficiency, the government will have to take upon the mantle of the Middle man between foreign businesses and merchants in Guangzhou, providing necessary oversight and rational direction to the investments coming in from abroad.
Cantonese Trade Ties
Guangzhou, the City of Flowers, is tremendously important as a trading hub, and by leveraging trade ties between it and Yunnan, economic development would be rapidly accelerated. China's economy will be uplifted not through reliance on foreign imperialists, but through the strong ties and friendship between the Chinese provinces themselves.
Southern Interregional Trade
In order to fully utilise the economic potential of southern China, economic integration must be promoted. Several notable strides towards integrating Kunming and Guangzhou have already been made, and now, it is time to accelerate and bring this process to its natural conclusion. Yunnan will find its way through modernity by making itself indispensable in the Chinese domestic economy!
A Tale of Two Cities
Under new management, Kunming has truly become one of the most important economic regions in China, and with further cooperation and integration with Guangzhou, new ground in Chinese interregional trade has been broken, and economic progress is better than ever before. Long may China prosper!
Invest in Mining
In Yunnan, tin exports have already been expanded, and smelting is now done locally. However, this is not enough: For Yunnan, and by extension China, to continue its unshaken march to modernity, the mining industry must be developed further. Yunnan's reserves are as rich as ever, all that is needed now to do is tap even further into them. Gejiu will dominate not just the markets of Asia, but those of Europe as well!
Rural Demilitarisation
Governor Tang Jiyao saw the issues that lay in Yunnan's unending militarisation, and attempted to combat it with several measures. Now, however, the [ROOT.GetLeader] Administration will go even further. Demilitarisation seeks to undo rural devastation, one of the worst side effects of Cai E's militarist programme. The demilitarisation of rural regions at last will go a long way towards relieving the populace and restoring hope in a better economic future.
Balance the Budget
Excessive military spending almost drove Yunnan's economy to utter ruin by creating an increasingly skewed budget and accruing crippling debt for the province. The time has come to cut down on the incessant funding of the military, and focus on paying off debts. Without a properly balanced budget, the door to modernity will remain forever closed.
Guangzhou Kunming Railway
It is time for Chinese interregional trade to be modernised even further: With the construction of a brand new railway between Guangzhou and Kunming, more trade will now go through the former, making us naturally less reliant on the Germans. With this new development, Yunnan will finally end decades of economic subjugation.
A Prosperous South
After decades of militarism, Southern China finds itself on a new path. While once Yunnan was known for being the great martial province of the south alongside Guangxi, Kunming now shows itself in a new light of peaceful modernity. While much of China remains in chaos and ravaged by war, Southern China turns a new leaf, an example for the rest of the nation!
Backbone of the South
All throughout China's existence, agriculture has consistently played a role of utmost importance. Chinese civilisation sprung out of the Yellow River, derived from wheat farming. When China expanded into the south, rice farming was added into Chinese agriculture. For millennia, agriculture has stood as the primary pillar of the Chinese people, and thus its reformation into the modern era stands as our top priority.
Mechanising Agriculture
In the modern age, an agricultural sector is nothing without the proper mechanical tools. New industrial machines greatly increase output, and China too must harness the power of mechanised agriculture if the nation is to compete with the Great Powers. No longer will Chinese agriculture rely purely on human labour, for now our farmers must make use of motorised mechanisation.
Land to the Tiller
The ultimate goal of the National Revolution is to establish an equitable and revitalised Chinese state, with a citizenry infused with patriotic fervour. To that end, Chinese agriculture must not only be modernised, but equalised among the farming populace. Sun Yat-sen called for Land to the Tiller in his ambitious plans for the renewal of China, and with a Nationalist presence restored on the Mainland, the will of the eternal premier must be carried out.
Ending Rural Overtaxation
The rural areas have suffered greatly in decades past, with them sorely lacking in urbanisation and developmental programs and being constantly overtaxed. Many various taxation agencies were established to fund Kunming's agenda, but the lack of centralised control hampered responsible taxation. It is finally time to remedy this: A rural reconstruction campaign will be carried out across different regions, and the obscenely high taxes will be cut.
Material Reconstruction
The extent of the material damage done to the agricultural sector cannot be overstated. In order to raise it from the state of near-paralysis, significant material reconstruction efforts are necessary, and the government will act to provide them. Unending wars from the Warlord Era coupled with banditry have reduced the material wellbeing of the Yunnanese people to an all time low, but now the Nationalist Party will provide where Tang Jiyao could not.
Commercialising Agriculture
With the agricultural sector slowly coming back on track, new opportunities for economic growth are arising: One such opportunity is agricultural commercialisation. The abundance of tea and silk, in particular, can be sold for massive profit to greatly boost the economy. The commercialisation of agriculture on Chinese terms will allow wealth to not only flow into the hands of the government, but the people themselves.
Liberated Agriculture

The reforms of [ROOT.GetLeader]'s administration will not only uplift the people of the Southern China, but will become the inspiration for a nation-wide reform effort which will herald the end of the Century of Humiliation and the renewed rise of China on the world stage as an equal partner.

While the Yunnanese method has proven slow, it is a careful and long thought out process developed by decades of trial and error. The Opium Question
Ever since the 19th century, Opium has been a scourge on the people of Yunnan. Its pervasiveness in the Yunnanese economy makes it particularly troublesome to root out as well. Tang Jiyao tried many times to suppress the poppies, but in doing so it hurt the local economy and pushed many poverty stricken regions into anarchic states of banditry. Even now, the question remains unclear whether to restart opium suppression or merely institute taxation.

Grain is of utmost importance in Southern China, especially as famines constantly threaten food supply. Not only will the threat of famine which Sichuan suffers from in the present, but also will assist in the supply of the local armies.

By giving them incentives, private industry will develop, and the wealth provided to the state will allow for increased funding to government projects.
Land Reclamation Initiative
Land reclamation has remained an underutilised idea in Southern China, until now. Grain supply to the capital will be increased, and silk and rice production will hopefully increase. While the original bureau was less than successful, extra efforts will be put in to ensure that the renewed effort does not die out as well.
Yunnan's Course to Modernity
Yunnan is unique for the continuity of its reforms throughout its political history. The reformist agenda was passed from Li Jingxi to Cai E and Tang Jiyao. [ROOT.GetLeader] has worked tirelessly to maintain a long flowing method to reform, which although not as radical as the Nationalist agenda, will undoubtedly score a greater yield in the long term. The path to modernity will be achieved on the path of continuity, not aggressive rhetoric.

The National Revolution was born out of the call for action from the Party Reorganisation of 1924. The National Revolution was crippled in 1927, but now the Kuomintang has not only rebounded but triumphed. Long live the Republic of China!
